The **Moon** is [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth")'s only [natural satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_satellite "Natural satellite"). It [orbits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbit_of_the_Moon "Orbit of the Moon") around Earth at [an average distance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_distance "Lunar distance") of 384,399 kilometres (238,854 mi), about 30 times [Earth's diameter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_diameter "Earth diameter").[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-23) Its orbital period ([lunar month](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_month#Synodic_month "Lunar month")) and its rotation period ([lunar day](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_day "Lunar day")) are synchronized at 29.5 days by the pull of [Earth's gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity "Earth's gravity"). This makes the Moon[tidally locked](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidally_locked "Tidally locked")to Earth, always [facing it](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_side_of_the_Moon "Near side of the Moon")with the same side. The Moon's gravitational pull produces [tidal forces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_forces "Tidal forces") on Earth which are the main driver of Earth's [tides](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tide "Tide").

In [geophysical terms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysical_definition_of_planet "Geophysical definition of planet"), the Moon is a [planetary-mass object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_object "Planetary-mass object") or [satellite planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Satellite_planet "Satellite planet"). Its mass is 1.2% that of the Earth, and its diameter is 3,474 km (2,159 mi), roughly one-quarter of Earth's (about as wide as the [contiguous United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contiguous_United_States "Contiguous United States")). Within the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System"), it is the [largest and most massive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Solar_System_objects_by_size "List of Solar System objects by size") satellite in relation to its [parent planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_body "Primary body"). It is the fifth-largest and fifth-most massive moon overall, and is larger and more massive than all known [dwarf planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet "Dwarf planet").[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Metzger2021-24) Its [surface gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_gravity "Surface gravity") is about one-sixth of Earth's, about half that of [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars"), and the second-highest among all moons in the Solar System after [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter")'s moon [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon) "Io (moon)"). The body of the Moon is [differentiated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_differentiation "Planetary differentiation") and [terrestrial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_planet "Terrestrial planet"), with only a minuscule [hydrosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrosphere "Hydrosphere"), [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_the_Moon "Atmosphere of the Moon"), and [magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_the_Moon "Magnetic field of the Moon"). The [lunar surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_surface "Lunar surface") is covered in [lunar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_dust "Lunar dust") and marked by [mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_mountains_on_the_Moon "List of mountains on the Moon"), [impact craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater"), [their ejecta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ejecta_blanket "Ejecta blanket"), [ray-like streaks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_system "Ray system"), [rilles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rille "Rille") and, mostly on the near side of the Moon, by dark [maria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare") ('seas'), which are plains of [cooled lava](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basalt "Basalt"). These maria are believed to have formed when molten lava flowed into ancient impact basins. It is thought that the [Moon was formed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_the_Moon "Origin of the Moon") 4.51 billion years ago, not long after [Earth's formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Age_of_Earth "Age of Earth"), out of the debris from [a giant impact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant-impact_hypothesis "Giant-impact hypothesis") between Earth and a hypothesized Mars-sized body named [Theia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theia_(planet) "Theia (planet)").

From a distance, the day and night phases of the lunar day are visible as the [lunar phases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_phases "Lunar phases"), and when the Moon passes through [Earth's shadow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_shadow "Earth's shadow") a [lunar eclipse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_eclipse "Lunar eclipse") is observable. The Moon's [apparent size](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_size "Apparent size") in Earth's sky is about the same as that of the Sun, which causes it to cover the Sun completely during a total [solar eclipse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_eclipse "Solar eclipse"). The Moon is the [brightest celestial object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_brightest_natural_objects_in_the_sky "List of brightest natural objects in the sky") in Earth's [night sky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Night_sky "Night sky") because of its large apparent size, while the [reflectance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflectance "Reflectance") (albedo) of its surface is comparable to that of [asphalt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asphalt_concrete "Asphalt concrete"). About 59% of the surface of the Moon is visible from Earth owing to the different angles at which the Moon can appear in Earth's sky ([libration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libration "Libration")), making parts of the [far side of the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon "Far side of the Moon") visible.

The Moon has been an important source of inspiration and knowledge in human history, having been crucial to [cosmography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmography "Cosmography"), mythology, [religion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_deity "Lunar deity"), art, [time keeping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_calendar "Lunar calendar"), [natural science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science "Natural science") and [spaceflight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceflight "Spaceflight"). The [first human-made objects to fly to an extraterrestrial body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_Solar_System_exploration "Timeline of Solar System exploration") were sent to the Moon, starting in 1959 with the flyby of the Soviet Union's _[Luna 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna\_1 "Luna 1")_ probe and the [intentional impact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impactor_(spacecraft) "Impactor (spacecraft)") of _[Luna 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna\_2 "Luna 2")_. In 1966, the first [soft landing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_landing "Soft landing") (by _[Luna 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna\_9 "Luna 9")_) and [orbital insertion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_insertion "Orbital insertion") (by _[Luna 10](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luna\_10 "Luna 10")_) followed. Humans arrived for the first time at the Moon, or any extraterrestrial body, in orbit on December 24, 1968, with [Apollo 8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_8 "Apollo 8") of the United States, and on the surface at [Mare Tranquillitatis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Tranquillitatis "Mare Tranquillitatis") on July 20, 1969, with the lander _[Eagle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar\_Module\_Eagle "Lunar Module Eagle")_ of [Apollo 11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_11 "Apollo 11"). By 1972, six Apollo missions had landed twelve humans on the Moon and stayed up to 75 hours. Renewed robotic [exploration of the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Moon "Exploration of the Moon"), in particular to confirm the presence of [water on the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_on_the_Moon "Water on the Moon"), has fueled plans to return humans to the Moon, starting with the [Artemis program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program "Artemis program") in the late 2020s.

Names and etymology
-------------------

The English proper name for Earth's natural satellite is typically written as _Moon_, with a capital _M_.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-25)[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-PN-FAQ-26) The noun _moon_ is derived from [Old English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English "Old English")_m≈çna_, which stems from [Proto-Germanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Germanic "Proto-Germanic")*_mƒìn≈çn_,[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-27) which in turn comes from [Proto-Indo-European](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Indo-European "Proto-Indo-European")_*mƒìnsis_ 'month'[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-28)‚Äì from earlier _*mƒìn≈çt_ (genitive _*mƒìneses_), which may be related to a verb meaning 'to measure [time]'.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-barnhart1995-29)

The Latin name for the Moon is _l≈´na_. The English adjective _lunar_ was ultimately borrowed from Latin, likely through French. In scientific writing and science fiction, the Moon is sometimes referred to as _Luna_[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-30) to distinguish it from other moons. In poetry, _Luna_ may also refer to the [personification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Personification "Personification") of the Moon as a woman.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-OEDLuna-31)

The Ancient Greek word _sel·∏ónƒì_ referred to the Moon as a celestial body, and also to the moon goddess [Selene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selene "Selene") . The rare English adjective _selenian_[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-32) is used to describe the Moon as a world, as opposed to a celestial object.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-33) Its cognate _selenic_, originally a rare synonym,[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-34) now almost always refers to the chemical element [selenium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selenium "Selenium").[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-35) The corresponding prefix _seleno-_ appears in terms including _[selenography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selenography "Selenography")_ (the study of the lunar surface).[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-oed-36)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-37)

[Artemis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis "Artemis"), the Greek goddess of the wilderness and the hunt, also came to be identified with Selene, and was sometimes called [Cynthia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cynthia "Cynthia") after her birthplace on [Mount Cynthus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mount_Cynthus "Mount Cynthus").[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Pannen2010-38) Her Roman equivalent is [Diana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diana_(mythology) "Diana (mythology)").

The [astronomical symbols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_symbols "Astronomical symbols") for the Moon are the crescent [![Image 4: ‚òΩ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Moon_crescent_symbol_%28bold%29.svg/24px-Moon_crescent_symbol_%28bold%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moon_crescent_symbol_(bold).svg "‚òΩ") and decrescent [![Image 5: ‚òæ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Moon_decrescent_symbol_%28bold%29.svg/24px-Moon_decrescent_symbol_%28bold%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moon_decrescent_symbol_(bold).svg "‚òæ"), for example in _M_‚òæ 'lunar mass'.

Natural history
---------------

### Formation

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Big_Splash_Theia.gif/250px-Big_Splash_Theia.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Big_Splash_Theia.gif)

This not to scale animation shows (in an Earth and Sun stationary rotating frame of reference) the formation of the Moon through the collision of the hypothetical Mars-sized planet named [Theia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theia_(hypothetical_planet) "Theia (hypothetical planet)") with Earth. After orbiting for tens of millions of years at the L 4[Lagrange point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_point "Lagrange point") as a Earth-[trojan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trojan_(astronomy) "Trojan (astronomy)"), Theia's orbit comes in "loops" finally too close to Earth.

[Isotope dating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope_dating "Isotope dating") of lunar samples suggests the Moon formed around 50 million years after the [origin of the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_of_the_Solar_System "Origin of the Solar System").[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-39)[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Age-40) Historically, several formation mechanisms have been proposed,[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-41) but none satisfactorily explains the features of the Earth‚ÄìMoon system. A fission of the Moon from Earth's crust through [centrifugal force](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifugal_force "Centrifugal force")[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Binder-42) would require too great an initial rotation rate of Earth.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-BotM-43) Gravitational capture of a pre-formed Moon[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Mitler-44) depends on an unfeasibly extended [atmosphere of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth") to [dissipate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissipate "Dissipate") the energy of the passing Moon.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-BotM-43) A co-formation of Earth and the Moon together in the [primordial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumplanetary_disk "Circumplanetary disk")[accretion disk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accretion_disk "Accretion disk") does not explain the depletion of metals in the Moon.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-BotM-43) None of these hypotheses can account for the high [angular momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_momentum "Angular momentum") of the Earth‚ÄìMoon system.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-45)

The prevailing theory is that the Earth‚ÄìMoon system formed after a [giant impact](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant_impact "Giant impact") of a [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars")-sized body (named _[Theia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theia\_(planet) "Theia (planet)")_) with the [proto-Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Earth "Proto-Earth"). The oblique impact blasted material into orbit about the Earth and the material accreted and formed the Moon[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-taylor1998-46)[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-47) just beyond the Earth's [Roche limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roche_limit "Roche limit") of ~2.56[_R_üú®](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth_radius "Earth radius").[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-48)

Giant impacts are thought to have been common in the early Solar System. Computer simulations of giant impacts have produced results that are consistent with the mass of the lunar core and the angular momentum of the Earth‚ÄìMoon system. These simulations show that most of the Moon derived from the impactor, rather than the proto-Earth.[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-49) However, models from 2007 and later suggest a larger fraction of the Moon derived from the proto-Earth.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-50)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-51)[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-52)[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-53) Other bodies of the inner Solar System such as Mars and [Vesta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4_Vesta "4 Vesta") have, according to meteorites from them, very different oxygen and tungsten [isotopic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotope "Isotope") compositions compared to Earth. However, Earth and the Moon have nearly identical isotopic compositions. The isotopic equalization of the Earth‚ÄìMoon system might be explained by the post-impact mixing of the vaporized material that formed the two,[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Pahlevan2007-54) although this is debated.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-55)

The impact would have released enough energy to liquefy both the ejecta and the Earth's crust, forming a magma ocean. The liquefied ejecta could have then re-accreted into the Earth‚ÄìMoon system.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Warren1985-56)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-57) The newly formed Moon would have had [its own magma ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_magma_ocean "Lunar magma ocean"); its depth is estimated from about 500 km (300 miles) to 1,737 km (1,079 miles).[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Warren1985-56)

While the giant-impact theory explains many lines of evidence, some questions are still unresolved, most of which involve the Moon's composition.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-58) Models that have the Moon acquiring a significant amount of the proto-Earth are more difficult to reconcile with geochemical data for the isotopes of zirconium, oxygen, silicon, and other elements.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-59) A study published in 2022, using high-resolution simulations (up to 10 8 particles), found that giant impacts can immediately place a satellite with similar mass and iron content to the Moon into orbit far outside Earth's [Roche limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roche_limit "Roche limit"). Even satellites that initially pass within the Roche limit can reliably and predictably survive, by being partially stripped and then torqued onto wider, stable orbits.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-60)

On November 1, 2023, scientists reported that, according to computer simulations, remnants of Theia could still be present inside the Earth.[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-NYT-20231101-61)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-NAT-20231101-62)

### Natural development

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Archean.png/250px-Archean.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Archean.png)

Artist's depiction of the Moon in Earth's sky as it might have appeared around 4 billion years ago, after the [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment"). At that time the Moon orbited the Earth at a third of its current distance, making it appear 2.8 times larger in [apparent size](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_size "Apparent size") than it does today.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Lunar_and_Planetary_Institute-63)

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Space.0076.fig.002.jpg/250px-Space.0076.fig.002.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Space.0076.fig.002.jpg)

The Moon was after its formation for billions of years volcanically active, with lava flooding the lunar low lying terrane over time forming today's basalt mare, with their ages marked in this map (yellow to red: youngest; blue to purple: oldest).

The newly formed Moon settled into a much closer Earth orbit than it has today. Each body therefore appeared much larger in the sky of the other, [eclipses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eclipse "Eclipse") were more frequent, and [tidal effects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_effect "Tidal effect") were stronger.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Lunar_and_Planetary_Institute-63) Due to [tidal acceleration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_acceleration "Tidal acceleration"), the Moon's orbit around Earth has become significantly larger, with a longer period.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-64)

Following formation, the Moon has cooled and most of [its atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_the_Moon "Atmosphere of the Moon") has been stripped.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-John_2017-65) The [lunar surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_of_the_Moon "Surface of the Moon") has since been shaped by large [impact events](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_event "Impact event") and many small ones, forming a landscape featuring [craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_crater "Lunar crater") of all ages.

The Moon was [volcanically active](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism "Volcanism") until 1.2 billion years ago, which laid down the prominent [lunar maria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_maria "Lunar maria"). Most of the [mare basalts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare") erupted during the [Imbrian period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imbrian "Imbrian"), 3.3‚Äì3.7 billion years ago, though some are as young as 1.2 billion years[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Hiesinger-66) and some as old as 4.2 billion years.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Papike-67) The distibution of the mare basalts is uneven, with the basalts predominantly appearing on the Moon's near-side hemisphere. The reasons for this are not yet known, although the relative thinness of the crust on the near side of the Moon is hypothesized to be a factor.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-68) Causes of the distribution of the [lunar highlands](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_highlands "Lunar highlands") on the [far side](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon "Far side of the Moon") are also not well understood. Topological measurements show the near side crust is thinner than the far side. One possible scenario then is that large impacts on the near side may have made it easier for lava to flow onto the surface.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-ESA_Science_&_Technology_2006-69)

### Lunar geologic timescale

![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/rqgvwx9mhj95lj57eva0a98t8ypxloi.png)

Millions of years before present

The lunar geological periods are named after their characteristic features, from most [impact craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater") outside the dark [mare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare"), to the mare and later craters, and finally the young, still bright and therefore readily visible craters with [ray systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_system "Ray system") like [Copernicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernicus_(lunar_crater) "Copernicus (lunar crater)") or [Tycho](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tycho_(lunar_crater) "Tycho (lunar crater)").

Physical characteristics
------------------------

The Moon is a very slightly scalene [ellipsoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellipsoid "Ellipsoid") due to tidal stretching, with its long axis displaced 30¬∞ from facing the Earth, due to gravitational anomalies from impact basins. Its shape is more elongated than current tidal forces can account for. This 'fossil bulge' indicates that the Moon solidified when it orbited at half its current distance to the Earth, and that it is now too cold for its shape to restore [hydrostatic equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium") at its current orbital distance.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-70)

### Size and mass

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg/500px-Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moons_of_solar_system_v7.jpg)

Size comparison of the main moons of the Solar System with Earth to scale. Nineteen moons are large enough [to be round](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium"), several having [subsurface oceans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subsurface_ocean "Subsurface ocean") and one, Titan, having a considerable atmosphere.

The Moon is by size and mass the fifth largest natural satellite of the Solar System, categorizable as one of its [planetary-mass moons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary-mass_moon "Planetary-mass moon"), making it a satellite planet under the [geophysical definitions of the term](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geophysical_definition_of_planet "Geophysical definition of planet").[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Metzger2021-24) It is smaller than [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)") but considerably larger than the largest [dwarf planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_planet "Dwarf planet") of the Solar System, [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto"). The Moon is the largest natural satellite in the Solar System relative to its [primary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primary_(astronomy) "Primary (astronomy)") planet.[[g]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-71)[[h]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Moon_vs._Charon-72)[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-73)

The Moon's diameter is about 3,500 km, more than one-quarter of Earth's, with the face of the Moon comparable to the width of either [mainland Australia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mainland_Australia "Mainland Australia"),[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Australia-74) Europe or the [contiguous United States](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contiguous_United_States "Contiguous United States").[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Dyches_2021_d923-75) The whole surface area of the Moon is about 38 million square kilometers, comparable to that of the [Americas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas "Americas").[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-y857-76)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-f708-77)

The Moon's mass is 1‚ÅÑ81 of Earth's,[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-worldbook-78) being the second densest among the planetary moons, and having the second highest [surface gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_gravity "Surface gravity"), after [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon) "Io (moon)"), at 0.1654[g](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-force "G-force") and an escape velocity of 2.38 km/s (8 600 km/h; 5 300 mph).

### Structure

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Return_of_the_moon_diagram.svg/500px-Return_of_the_moon_diagram.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Return_of_the_moon_diagram.svg)

Moon's internal structure: solid inner core (iron-metallic), molten outer core, hardened mantle and crust. The crust on the Moon's near side permanently facing Earth is thinner, featuring larger areas flooded by material of the once molten mantle forming today's [lunar mare](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare").

The Moon is a [differentiated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_differentiation "Planetary differentiation") body that was initially in [hydrostatic equilibrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium "Hydrostatic equilibrium") but has since departed from this condition.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-79) It has a [geochemically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geochemically "Geochemically") distinct [crust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crust_(geology) "Crust (geology)"), [mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(geology) "Mantle (geology)"), and [core](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planetary_core "Planetary core"). The Moon has a solid iron-rich inner core with a radius possibly as small as 240 kilometres (150 mi) and a fluid outer core primarily made of liquid iron with a radius of roughly 300 kilometres (190 mi). Around the core is a partially molten boundary layer with a radius of about 500 kilometres (310 mi).[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-80)[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Weber2011-81) This structure is thought to have developed through the [fractional crystallization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fractional_crystallization_(geology) "Fractional crystallization (geology)") of a global magma ocean shortly after the Moon's formation 4.5 billion years ago.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-82)

Crystallization of this magma ocean would have created a [mafic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mafic "Mafic") mantle from the [precipitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precipitation_(chemistry) "Precipitation (chemistry)") and sinking of the minerals [olivine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olivine "Olivine"), [clinopyroxene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clinopyroxene "Clinopyroxene"), and [orthopyroxene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthopyroxene "Orthopyroxene"); after about three-quarters of the magma ocean had crystallized, lower-density [plagioclase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plagioclase "Plagioclase") minerals could form and float into a crust atop.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-S06-83) The final liquids to crystallize would have been initially sandwiched between the crust and mantle, with a high abundance of [incompatible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compatibility_(geochemistry) "Compatibility (geochemistry)") and heat-producing elements.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-W06-1) Consistent with this perspective, geochemical mapping made from orbit suggests a crust of mostly [anorthosite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anorthosite "Anorthosite").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-L06-21) The [Moon rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_rock "Moon rock") samples of the flood lavas that erupted onto the surface from partial melting in the mantle confirm the mafic mantle composition, which is more iron-rich than that of Earth.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-W06-1) The crust is on average about 50 kilometres (31 mi) thick.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-W06-1)

The Moon is the second-densest satellite in the Solar System, after [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon) "Io (moon)").[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Schubert2004-84) However, the inner core of the Moon is small, with a radius of about 350 kilometres (220 mi) or less,[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-W06-1) around 20% of the radius of the Moon. Its composition is not well understood but is probably metallic iron alloyed with a small amount of sulfur and nickel. Analyses of the Moon's time-variable rotation suggest that it is at least partly molten.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-85) The pressure at the lunar core is estimated to be 5 GPa (49,000 atm).[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-86)

### Gravitational field

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Youtubeastronautsonmoonot3.gif/250px-Youtubeastronautsonmoonot3.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Youtubeastronautsonmoonot3.gif)

Astronaut [John Young](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Young_(astronaut) "John Young (astronaut)") jumping on the Moon, illustrating that the [gravitational pull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration "Gravitational acceleration") of the Moon is approximately 1/6 of Earth's. The jumping height is limited by the EVA space suit's weight on the Moon of about 13.6 kg (30 lb) and by the suit's pressurization resisting the bending of the suit, as needed for jumping.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Kluger_2018_z081-87)[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Nast_2013_v237-88)

On average the Moon's [surface gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_gravity "Surface gravity") is 1.62[m/s 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared "Metre per second squared")[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-NSSDC-7) (0.1654[g](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G-force "G-force"); 5.318[ft/s 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foot_per_second_squared "Foot per second squared")), about half of the surface gravity of [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars") and about a sixth of Earth's.

The [Moon's gravitational field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitation_of_the_Moon "Gravitation of the Moon") is not uniform. The details of the gravitational field have been measured through tracking the [Doppler shift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_shift "Doppler shift") of radio signals emitted by orbiting spacecraft. The main lunar gravity features are [mascons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_concentration_(astronomy) "Mass concentration (astronomy)"), large positive gravitational anomalies associated with some of the giant impact basins, partly caused by the dense mare basaltic lava flows that fill those basins.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-89)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-90) The anomalies greatly influence the orbit of spacecraft about the Moon. There are some puzzles: lava flows by themselves cannot explain all of the gravitational signature, and some mascons exist that are not linked to mare volcanism.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-91)

The [sphere of influence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphere_of_influence_(astronomy) "Sphere of influence (astronomy)"), of the Moon's gravity field, in which it dominates over Earth's has a [Hill radius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hill_sphere "Hill sphere") of 60,000 km (i.e., extending less than one-sixth the distance of the 378,000 km between the Moon and the Earth),[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-92) extending to the Earth-Moon [lagrange points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrange_points "Lagrange points"). This space is called [cislunar space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cislunar_space "Cislunar space").[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-v692-93)

### Magnetic field

The Moon has [an external magnetic field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field_of_the_Moon "Magnetic field of the Moon") of less than 0.2 [nanoteslas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tesla_(unit) "Tesla (unit)"),[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Mighani2020-94) or less than one hundred thousandth [that of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field "Earth's magnetic field"). The Moon does not have a global [dipolar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dipolar "Dipolar") magnetic field and only has crustal magnetization likely acquired early in its history when a dynamo was still operating.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-GB2009-95)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-96) Early in its history, 4 billion years ago, its magnetic field strength was likely close to that of Earth today.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-v735-97)[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Mighani2020-94) This early dynamo field apparently expired by about one billion years ago, after the lunar core had crystallized.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Mighani2020-94) Theoretically, some of the remnant magnetization may originate from transient magnetic fields generated during large impacts through the expansion of plasma clouds. These clouds are generated during large impacts in an ambient magnetic field. This is supported by the location of the largest crustal magnetizations situated near the [antipodes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antipodes "Antipodes") of the giant impact basins.[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-98)

Additionally the Moon moves ~27% of the time,[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-z266-99) or 5‚Äì6 days per lunar month in Earth's [magnetotail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetotail "Magnetotail"), replacing [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind") with [Earth wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Earth_wind&action=edit&redlink=1 "Earth wind (page does not exist)").[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-u789-100)

### Atmosphere

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/20120927_surveyor7-levitating-dust.jpg/250px-20120927_surveyor7-levitating-dust.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:20120927_surveyor7-levitating-dust.jpg)

[Surveyor 7](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surveyor_7 "Surveyor 7") observes levitating dust, a phenomenon named [Lunar horizon glow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_horizon_glow "Lunar horizon glow") can be seen

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/11214_2022_871_Fig6_HTML.webp/250px-11214_2022_871_Fig6_HTML.webp.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:11214_2022_871_Fig6_HTML.webp)

Observations and modelling of the [Moon‚Äôs sodium exosphere, tail](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium_tail_of_the_Moon "Sodium tail of the Moon") and ‚Äúspot‚Äù.

The Moon has an [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere "Atmosphere") consisting of only an [exosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exosphere "Exosphere"),[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-l546-101) which is so tenuous as to be nearly [vacuum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacuum "Vacuum"), with a total mass of less than 10 tonnes (9.8 long tons; 11 short tons).[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-102) The surface pressure of this small mass is around 3 √ó 10‚àí15[atm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit) "Atmosphere (unit)") (0.3[nPa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanopascal "Nanopascal")); it varies with the lunar day. Its sources include [outgassing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outgassing "Outgassing") and [sputtering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sputtering "Sputtering"), a product of the bombardment of lunar soil by solar wind ions.[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-L06-21)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-103) Elements that have been detected include [sodium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sodium "Sodium") and [potassium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potassium "Potassium"), produced by sputtering (also found in the atmospheres of Mercury and [Io](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Io_(moon) "Io (moon)")); [helium-4](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-4 "Helium-4") and [neon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neon "Neon")[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-NASA-20150817-104) from the solar wind; and [argon-40](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argon "Argon"), [radon-222](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radon "Radon"), and [polonium-210](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polonium-210 "Polonium-210"), outgassed after their creation by [radioactive decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_decay "Radioactive decay") within the crust and mantle.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Stern1999-105)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-106) The absence of such neutral species (atoms or molecules) as [oxygen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxygen "Oxygen"), [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen"), [carbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon "Carbon"), [hydrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen "Hydrogen") and [magnesium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium "Magnesium"), which are present in the [regolith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regolith "Regolith"), is not understood.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Stern1999-105) Water vapor has been detected by _[Chandrayaan-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandrayaan-1 "Chandrayaan-1")_ and found to vary with latitude, with a maximum at ~60‚Äì70 degrees; it is possibly generated from the [sublimation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry) "Sublimation (chemistry)") of water ice in the regolith.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Sridharan2010-107) These gases either return into the regolith because of the Moon's gravity or are lost to space, either through solar radiation pressure or, if they are ionized, by being swept away by the solar wind's magnetic field.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Stern1999-105)

A permanent [Moon dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_dust "Moon dust") cloud exists around the Moon, generated by small particles from comets. 5 tons of comet particles are estimated to strike the Moon's surface every 24 hours, resulting in the ejection of dust particles. The dust stays above the Moon for approximately 10 minutes, taking 5 minutes to rise, and 5 minutes to fall. On average, 120 kilograms of dust are present above the Moon, rising up to 100 kilometers above the surface. Dust counts made by [LADEE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LADEE "LADEE")'s Lunar Dust EXperiment (LDEX) found particle counts peaked during the [Geminid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geminid "Geminid"), [Quadrantid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quadrantid "Quadrantid"), [Northern Taurid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taurids "Taurids"), and [Omicron Centaurid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omicron_Centaurids "Omicron Centaurids")[meteor showers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteor_shower "Meteor shower"), when the Earth, and Moon pass through comet debris. The lunar dust cloud is asymmetric, being denser near the boundary between the Moon's dayside and nightside.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-108)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-109)

Studies of Moon magma samples retrieved by the [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_program "Apollo program") missions demonstrate that the Moon had once possessed a relatively thick atmosphere for a period of 70 million years between 3 and 4 billion years ago. This atmosphere, sourced from gases ejected from lunar volcanic eruptions, was twice the thickness of that of present-day [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars"). The ancient lunar atmosphere was eventually stripped away by solar winds and dissipated into space.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-John_2017-65)

### Surface conditions

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/AS17-145-22224.jpg/250px-AS17-145-22224.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:AS17-145-22224.jpg)

[Gene Cernan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_Cernan "Gene Cernan") with [lunar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_dust "Lunar dust") stuck on his suit. Lunar dust is highly abrasive and can cause damage to human lungs and nervous and cardiovascular systems.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-110)

[Ionizing radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ionizing_radiation "Ionizing radiation") from [cosmic rays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_ray "Cosmic ray"), their resulting [neutron radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron_radiation "Neutron radiation"),[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Science_Mission_Directorate_2005-111) and the Sun results in an average radiation level of 1.369 [millisieverts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Millisievert "Millisievert") per day during lunar [daytime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daytime "Daytime"),[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-surface-radiation-17) which is about 2.6 times more than the level on the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station"), 5‚Äì10 times more than the level during a trans-Atlantic flight, and 200 times more than the level on Earth's surface.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-ScienceAlert_2020-112) For further comparison, radiation levels average about 1.84 millisieverts per day on a [flight to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars") and about 0.64 millisieverts per day on Mars itself, with some locations on Mars possibly having levels as low as 0.342 millisieverts per day.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Paris_Davies_Tognetti_Zahniser_2020-113)[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Wall_2013-114) Solar radiation also [electrically charges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Static_electricity "Static electricity") the highly abrasive [lunar dust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_dust "Lunar dust") and makes it levitate. This effect contributes to the easy spread of the sticky, lung- and gear-damaging lunar dust.[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-e657-115)

The Moon's [axial tilt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axial_tilt "Axial tilt") with respect to the [ecliptic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecliptic "Ecliptic") is only 1.5427¬∞,[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-SolarViews-11)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-116) much less than the 23.44¬∞ of Earth. This small axial tilt means that the Moon's solar illumination varies much less with [season](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Season "Season") than Earth's, and it also allows for the existence of some [peaks of eternal light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peaks_of_eternal_light "Peaks of eternal light") at the [Moon's north pole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_north_pole "Lunar north pole"), at the rim of the crater [Peary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peary_(crater) "Peary (crater)").

The lunar surface is exposed to drastic temperature differences ranging from 120¬∞C to ‚àí171¬∞C depending on the [solar irradiance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_irradiance "Solar irradiance"). Because of the lack of atmosphere, temperatures of different areas vary particularly upon whether they are in sunlight or shadow,[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-117) making topographical details play a decisive role on local [surface temperatures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_surface_temperature "Global surface temperature").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-bbc-118) Parts of many craters, particularly the bottoms of many polar craters,[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-M03-119) are permanently shadowed. These [craters of eternal darkness](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crater_of_eternal_darkness "Crater of eternal darkness") have extremely low temperatures. The _Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter_ measured the lowest summer temperatures in craters at the southern pole at 35 K (‚àí238¬∞C; ‚àí397¬∞F)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-120) and just 26 K (‚àí247¬∞C; ‚àí413¬∞F) close to the winter solstice in the north polar crater [Hermite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermite_(crater) "Hermite (crater)"). This is the coldest temperature in the Solar System ever measured by a spacecraft, colder even than the surface of [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto").[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-bbc-118)

Blanketed on top of the Moon's crust is a highly [comminuted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comminution "Comminution") (broken into ever smaller particles) and [impact gardened](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_gardening "Impact gardening") mostly gray surface layer called [regolith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regolith "Regolith"), formed by impact processes. The finer regolith, the [lunar soil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_soil "Lunar soil") of [silicon dioxide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silicon_dioxide "Silicon dioxide") glass, has a texture resembling snow and a scent resembling spent [gunpowder](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gunpowder "Gunpowder").[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-121) The regolith of older surfaces is generally thicker than for younger surfaces: it varies in thickness from 10‚Äì15 m (33‚Äì49 ft) in the highlands and 4‚Äì5 m (13‚Äì16 ft) in the maria.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-122) Beneath the finely comminuted regolith layer is the megaregolith, a layer of highly fractured bedrock many kilometers thick.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-123)

These extreme conditions are considered to make it unlikely for spacecraft to harbor bacterial spores at the Moon for longer than just one lunar orbit.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-124)

### Surface features

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c6/Near_and_far_side_of_the_Moon.jpg/330px-Near_and_far_side_of_the_Moon.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Near_and_far_side_of_the_Moon.jpg)

The near side (left) and far side (right) of the Moon in visible light.

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Moon_crustal_thickness_map_NASA-JPL.jpg/330px-Moon_crustal_thickness_map_NASA-JPL.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moon_crustal_thickness_map_NASA-JPL.jpg)

Map of the Moon coloured by the thickness of the crust, with main regions marked.The [KREEP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KREEP "KREEP") rich magma terrane (hatched), with its most prominent part labeled on the near side (left), the [Mare Procellarum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Procellarum "Mare Procellarum") KREEP Terrane (PKT).The [South Pole-Aitken Terrane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Pole-Aitken_basin "South Pole-Aitken basin") (SPAT) is marked on the far side (right, largest circle).The 24 crater basins with crustal thinning greater than 200 km (120 mi) are marked (black circles) across both hemispheres.The rest is the high-thickness [Felspathic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felspathic "Felspathic")Highlands Terrane (FHT; red and white).[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-125)

The [topography of the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topography_of_the_Moon "Topography of the Moon") has been measured with [laser altimetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laser_altimetry "Laser altimetry") and [stereo image analysis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stereoscopy "Stereoscopy").[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-126) Its most extensive [topographic feature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_features_on_the_Moon "List of features on the Moon") is the giant far-side [South Pole‚ÄìAitken basin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Pole%E2%80%93Aitken_basin "South Pole‚ÄìAitken basin"), some 2,240 km (1,390 mi) in diameter, the largest crater on the Moon and the second-largest confirmed impact [crater in the Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_largest_craters_in_the_Solar_System "List of largest craters in the Solar System").[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Spudis1994-127)[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-128) At 13 km (8.1 mi) deep, its floor is the lowest point on the surface of the Moon,[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Spudis1994-127)[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-129) reaching ‚àí9.178 kilometres (‚àí5.703 mi) at [70¬∞22‚Ä≤05‚Ä≥S 172¬∞24‚Ä≤47‚Ä≥W / 70.368¬∞S 172.413¬∞W](https://geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Moon&params=70.368_S_172.413_W_globe:moon_type:landmark) in a crater within [Antoniadi crater](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antoniadi_(lunar_crater) "Antoniadi (lunar crater)").[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-n402-130) The [highest elevations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_tallest_mountains_in_the_Solar_System "List of tallest mountains in the Solar System") of the Moon's surface, with the so-called [Selenean summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selenean_summit "Selenean summit") at 10.629 kilometres (6.605 mi) , are located directly to the northeast ([5¬∞26‚Ä≤28‚Ä≥N 158¬∞39‚Ä≤22‚Ä≥W / 5.441¬∞N 158.656¬∞W](https://geohack.toolforge.org/geohack.php?pagename=Moon&params=5.441_N_158.656_W_globe:moon_type:landmark)),[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-n402-130) which might have been thickened by the oblique formation impact of the South Pole‚ÄìAitken basin.[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-131) Other large impact basins such as [Imbrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Imbrium "Mare Imbrium"), [Serenitatis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Serenitatis "Mare Serenitatis"), [Crisium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Crisium "Mare Crisium"), [Smythii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Smythii "Mare Smythii"), and [Orientale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Orientale "Mare Orientale") possess regionally low elevations and elevated rims.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Spudis1994-127) The far side of the lunar surface is on average about 1.9 km (1.2 mi) higher than that of the near side.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-W06-1)

The discovery of [fault scarp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fault_scarp "Fault scarp") cliffs suggest that the Moon has shrunk by about 90 metres (300 ft) within the past billion years.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-132) Similar shrinkage features exist on [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geology_of_Mercury "Geology of Mercury"). Mare Frigoris, a basin near the north pole long assumed to be geologically dead, has cracked and shifted. Since the Moon does not have tectonic plates, its tectonic activity is slow, and cracks develop as it loses heat.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-133)

Scientists have confirmed the presence of a cave on the Moon near the [Sea of Tranquillity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sea_of_Tranquillity "Sea of Tranquillity"), not far from the 1969 [Apollo 11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_11 "Apollo 11") landing site. The cave, identified as an entry point to a collapsed [lava tube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_lava_tube "Lunar lava tube"), is roughly 45 meters wide and up to 80 m long. This discovery marks the first confirmed entry point to a lunar cave. The analysis was based on photos taken in 2010 by NASA's [Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Reconnaissance_Orbiter "Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter"). The cave's stable temperature of around 17¬∞C could provide a hospitable environment for future astronauts, protecting them from extreme temperatures, solar radiation, and micrometeorites. However, challenges include accessibility and risks of avalanches and cave-ins. This discovery offers potential for future lunar bases or emergency shelters.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-134)

#### Volcanic features

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Moon_names.svg/330px-Moon_names.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Moon_names.svg)

Map of the near side of the Moon, with the dark [lunar maria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare"), the most prominent volcanic features of the Moon, labeled in blue (brown labels are prominent [lunar craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_craters "Lunar craters"))

The main features visible from Earth by the naked eye are dark and relatively featureless lunar plains called _[maria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar\_mare "Lunar mare")_ (sing._mare_; from Latin meaning 'seas', as they were once believed to be filled with water)[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-135) which are vast solidified pools of ancient [basaltic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basalt "Basalt") lava. Although similar to terrestrial basalts, lunar basalts have more iron and no minerals altered by water.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-136) The majority of these lava deposits erupted or flowed into the depressions associated with [impact basins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_basins "Impact basins"), though the Moon's largest expanse of basalt flooding, [Oceanus Procellarum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanus_Procellarum "Oceanus Procellarum"), does not correspond to an obvious impact basin. Different episodes of lava flow in maria can often be recognized by variations in surface albedo and distinct flow margins.[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-137)

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Lava_flows_in_Mare_Imbrium_%28AS15-M-1558%29.png/250px-Lava_flows_in_Mare_Imbrium_%28AS15-M-1558%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lava_flows_in_Mare_Imbrium_(AS15-M-1558).png)

Old [hardened](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basalt "Basalt") lava flows of [Mare Imbrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Imbrium "Mare Imbrium") forming [wrinkle ridges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wrinkle_ridge "Wrinkle ridge")

As the maria formed, cooling and contraction of the basaltic lava created [wrinkle ridges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wrinkle_ridge "Wrinkle ridge") in some areas. These low, sinuous ridges can extend for hundreds of kilometers and often outline buried structures within the mare. Another result of maria formation is the creation of concentric depressions along the edges, known as [arcuate rilles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rille "Rille"). These features occur as the mare basalts sink inward under their own weight, causing the edges to fracture and separate.

In addition to the visible maria, the Moon has mare deposits covered by ejecta from impacts. Called cryptomares, these hidden mares are likely older than the exposed ones.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-138) Conversely, mare lava has obscured many impact melt sheets and pools. Impact melts are formed when intense shock pressures from collisions vaporize and melt zones around the impact site. Where still exposed, impact melt can be distinguished from mare lava by its distribution, albedo, and texture.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-139)

[Sinuous rilles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinuous_rilles "Sinuous rilles"), found in and around maria, are likely extinct [lava channels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lava_channels "Lava channels") or collapsed [lava tubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lava_tubes "Lava tubes"). They typically originate from volcanic [vents](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism_on_the_Moon "Volcanism on the Moon"), meandering and sometimes branching as they progress. The largest examples, such as [Schroter's Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schroter%27s_Valley "Schroter's Valley") and [Rima Hadley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadley%E2%80%93Apennine "Hadley‚ÄìApennine"), are significantly longer, wider, and deeper than terrestrial lava channels, sometimes featuring bends and sharp turns that again, are uncommon on Earth.

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Marius_Hills_Pit_-_Lava_Tube_Skylight%3F_%28LROC202_-_KaguyaTerrainCamera_039%29.png/250px-Marius_Hills_Pit_-_Lava_Tube_Skylight%3F_%28LROC202_-_KaguyaTerrainCamera_039%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Marius_Hills_Pit_-_Lava_Tube_Skylight%3F_(LROC202_-_KaguyaTerrainCamera_039).png)

[Sinuous rilles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinuous_rilles "Sinuous rilles") features and [lunar lava tube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_lava_tube "Lunar lava tube") cave _Marius Hills pit_ as observed under different solar illumination conditions.

Mare volcanism has altered impact craters in various ways, including filling them to varying degrees, and raising and fracturing their floors from uplift of mare material beneath their interiors. Examples of such craters include [Taruntius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taruntius_(crater) "Taruntius (crater)") and [Gassendi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gassendi_(crater) "Gassendi (crater)"). Some craters, such as [Hyginus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyginus_(crater) "Hyginus (crater)"), are of wholly volcanic origin, forming as [calderas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caldera "Caldera") or [collapse pits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pit_crater "Pit crater"). Such craters are relatively rare and tend to be smaller (typically a few kilometers wide), shallower, and more irregularly shaped than impact craters. They also lack the upturned rims characteristic of impact craters.

Several [geologic provinces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geologic_province "Geologic province") containing [shield volcanoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shield_volcano "Shield volcano") and volcanic [domes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_dome "Lunar dome") are found within the near side maria.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-140) There are also some regions of [pyroclastic deposits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyroclastic_rock "Pyroclastic rock"), [scoria cones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scoria_cones "Scoria cones") and [non-basaltic domes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volcanism_on_the_Moon "Volcanism on the Moon") made of particularly high viscosity lava.

Almost all maria are on the near side of the Moon, and cover 31% of the surface of the near side[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-worldbook-78) compared with 2% of the far side.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-141) This is likely due to a [concentration of heat-producing elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/KREEP "KREEP") under the crust on the near side, which would have caused the underlying mantle to heat up, partially melt, rise to the surface and erupt.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-S06-83)[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-142)[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-143) Most of the Moon's [mare basalts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_mare "Lunar mare") erupted during the [Imbrian period](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Imbrian "Imbrian"), 3.3‚Äì3.7 billion years ago, though some are as young as 1.2 billion years[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Hiesinger-66) and as old as 4.2 billion years.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Papike-67)

In 2006, a study of [Ina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ina_(crater) "Ina (crater)"), a tiny depression in [Lacus Felicitatis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lacus_Felicitatis "Lacus Felicitatis"), found jagged, relatively dust-free features that, because of the lack of erosion by infalling debris, appeared to be only 2 million years old.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Berardelli-144)[Moonquakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moonquake "Moonquake") and releases of gas indicate continued lunar activity.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Berardelli-144) Evidence of recent lunar volcanism has been identified at 70 [irregular mare patches](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irregular_mare_patch "Irregular mare patch"), some less than 50 million years old. This raises the possibility of a much warmer lunar mantle than previously believed, at least on the near side where the deep crust is substantially warmer because of the greater concentration of radioactive elements.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-145)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-146)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-147)[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-148) Evidence has been found for 2‚Äì10 million years old basaltic volcanism within the crater Lowell,[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-149)[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-150) inside the Orientale basin. Some combination of an initially hotter mantle and local enrichment of heat-producing elements in the mantle could be responsible for prolonged activities on the far side in the Orientale basin.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-151)[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-152)

The lighter-colored regions of the Moon are called _terrae_, or more commonly _highlands_, because they are higher than most maria. They have been radiometrically dated to having formed 4.4 billion years ago and may represent [plagioclase](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plagioclase "Plagioclase")[cumulates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cumulates "Cumulates") of the lunar magma ocean.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Hiesinger-66)[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Papike-67) In contrast to Earth, no major lunar mountains are believed to have formed as a result of tectonic events.[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-153)

The concentration of maria on the near side likely reflects the substantially thicker crust of the highlands of the Far Side, which may have formed in a slow-velocity impact of a second moon of Earth a few tens of millions of years after the Moon's formation.[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-154)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-155) Alternatively, it may be a consequence of asymmetrical [tidal heating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tidal_heating "Tidal heating") when the Moon was much closer to the Earth.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-156)

#### Impact craters

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Gibbous_Moon_Highlighting_the_Tycho_and_Copernicus_Craters.jpg/250px-Gibbous_Moon_Highlighting_the_Tycho_and_Copernicus_Craters.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gibbous_Moon_Highlighting_the_Tycho_and_Copernicus_Craters.jpg)

The heavily cratered surface of the Moon, particularly where no mare formed, is discernbale when viewed close up, with crater ridges highlighted at the [terminator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terminator_(solar) "Terminator (solar)") and the two most prominent craters on the near side, [Tycho](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tycho_(lunar_crater) "Tycho (lunar crater)") (left) and [Copernicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Copernicus_(lunar_crater) "Copernicus (lunar crater)") (top) visible, featuring long bright impact-streaks.

[![Image 22: A gray, 9feature is a circular ringed structure with high walled sides and a lower central peak: the entire surface out to the horizon is filled with similar structures that are smaller and overlapping.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Daedalus_crater_AS11-41-6151.jpg/250px-Daedalus_crater_AS11-41-6151.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Daedalus_crater_AS11-41-6151.jpg)

A view of a larger crater, the three-kilometer-deep [Daedalus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daedalus_(crater) "Daedalus (crater)") on the [Moon's far side](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_side_of_the_Moon "Far side of the Moon")

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Apollo_17_Harrison_H._Schmitt_and_Tracy%27s_Rock_-_AS17-140-21493%2BAS17-140-21497_2025.jpg/250px-Apollo_17_Harrison_H._Schmitt_and_Tracy%27s_Rock_-_AS17-140-21493%2BAS17-140-21497_2025.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apollo_17_Harrison_H._Schmitt_and_Tracy%27s_Rock_-_AS17-140-21493%2BAS17-140-21497_2025.jpg)

[Apollo 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_17 "Apollo 17") astronaut [Harrison H. Schmitt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harrison_H._Schmitt "Harrison H. Schmitt") next to the large Moon boulder nicknamed "[Tracy's Rock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tracy%27s_Rock "Tracy's Rock")" in the [Taurus‚ÄìLittrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taurus%E2%80%93Littrow "Taurus‚ÄìLittrow") of the [Mare Serenitatis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Serenitatis "Mare Serenitatis") on the near side of the Moon

A major geologic process that has affected the Moon's surface is [impact cratering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Impact_crater "Impact crater"),[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-157) with craters formed when asteroids and comets collide with the lunar surface. There are estimated to be roughly 300,000 craters wider than 1 km (0.6 mi) on the Moon's near side.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-158) Lunar craters exhibit a variety of forms, depending on their size. In order of increasing diameter, the basic types are simple craters with smooth bowl shaped interiors and upturned rims, [complex craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_crater "Complex crater") with flat floors, terraced walls and central peaks, [peak ring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peak_ring "Peak ring") basins, and [multi-ring basins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-ring_basin "Multi-ring basin") with two or more concentric rings of peaks.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-159) The vast majority of impact craters are circular, but some, like [Cantor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cantor_(crater) "Cantor (crater)") and [Janssen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Janssen_(lunar_crater) "Janssen (lunar crater)"), have more polygonal outlines, possibly guided by underlying faults and joints. Others, such as the [Messier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messier_(crater) "Messier (crater)") pair, [Schiller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schiller_(crater) "Schiller (crater)"), and [Daniell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniell_(crater) "Daniell (crater)"), are elongated. Such elongation can result from highly oblique impacts, [binary asteroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binary_asteroid "Binary asteroid") impacts, fragmentation of impactors before surface strike, or closely spaced [secondary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_crater "Secondary crater") impacts.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-160)

The [lunar geologic timescale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_geologic_timescale "Lunar geologic timescale") is based on the most prominent impact events, such as multi-ring formations like [Nectaris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nectarian "Nectarian"), [Imbrium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_Imbrian "Lower Imbrian"), and [Orientale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Orientale "Mare Orientale") that are between hundreds and thousands of kilometers in diameter and associated with a broad apron of ejecta deposits that form a regional [stratigraphic horizon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stratigraphy "Stratigraphy").[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-geologic-161) The lack of an atmosphere, weather, and recent geological processes mean that many of these craters are well-preserved. Although only a few [multi-ring basins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-ring_basins "Multi-ring basins") have been definitively dated, they are useful for assigning relative ages. Because impact craters accumulate at a nearly constant rate, counting the number of craters per unit area can be used to estimate the age of the surface.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-geologic-161) However care needs to be exercised with the [crater counting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crater_counting "Crater counting") technique due to the potential presence of [secondary craters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Secondary_crater "Secondary crater"). Ejecta from impacts can create secondary craters that often appear in clusters or chains but can also occur as isolated formations at a considerable distance from the impact. These can resemble primary craters, and may even dominate small crater populations, so their unidentified presence can distort age estimates.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-162)

The radiometric ages of impact-melted rocks collected during the [Apollo missions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_missions "Apollo missions") cluster between 3.8 and 4.1 billion years old: this has been used to propose a [Late Heavy Bombardment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment "Late Heavy Bombardment") period of increased impacts.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-163)

High-resolution images from the Lunar Reconnaissance Orbiter in the 2010s show a contemporary crater-production rate significantly higher than was previously estimated. A secondary cratering process caused by [distal ejecta](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distal_ejecta "Distal ejecta") is thought to churn the top two centimeters of regolith on a timescale of 81,000 years.[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-164)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-165) This rate is 100 times faster than the rate computed from models based solely on direct micrometeorite impacts.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-166)

#### Lunar swirls

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Reiner-gamma-clem1.jpg/250px-Reiner-gamma-clem1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Reiner-gamma-clem1.jpg)

Wide-angle image of a lunar swirl, the 70-kilometer-long [Reiner Gamma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reiner_Gamma "Reiner Gamma")

Lunar swirls are enigmatic features found across the Moon's surface. They are characterized by a high albedo, appear optically immature (i.e. the optical characteristics of a relatively young [regolith](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regolith "Regolith")), and often have a sinuous shape. Their shape is often accentuated by low [albedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo "Albedo") regions that wind between the bright swirls. They are located in places with enhanced surface [magnetic fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field "Magnetic field") and many are located at the [antipodal point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antipodal_point "Antipodal point") of major impacts. Well known swirls include the [Reiner Gamma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reiner_Gamma "Reiner Gamma") feature and [Mare Ingenii](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Ingenii "Mare Ingenii"). They are hypothesized to be areas that have been partially shielded from the [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind"), resulting in slower [space weathering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_weathering "Space weathering").[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-167)

### Presence of water

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/The_image_shows_the_distribution_of_surface_ice_at_the_Moon%27s_south_pole_%28left%29_and_north_pole_%28right%29.webp/330px-The_image_shows_the_distribution_of_surface_ice_at_the_Moon%27s_south_pole_%28left%29_and_north_pole_%28right%29.webp.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:The_image_shows_the_distribution_of_surface_ice_at_the_Moon%27s_south_pole_(left)_and_north_pole_(right).webp)

Distribution of surface ice at the Moon's South (left) and North poles (right), as viewed by NASA's [Moon Mineralogy Mapper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Mineralogy_Mapper "Moon Mineralogy Mapper") (M 3) spectrometer onboard India's [Chandrayaan-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandrayaan-1 "Chandrayaan-1") orbiter

Liquid water cannot persist on the lunar surface. When exposed to solar radiation, water quickly decomposes through a process known as [photodissociation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photodissociation "Photodissociation") and is lost to space. However, since the 1960s, scientists have hypothesized that water ice may be deposited by impacting [comets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comets "Comets") or possibly produced by the reaction of oxygen-rich lunar rocks, and hydrogen from [solar wind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_wind "Solar wind"), leaving traces of water which could possibly persist in cold, permanently shadowed craters at either pole on the Moon.[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Margot1999-168)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-169) Computer simulations suggest that up to 14,000 km 2 (5,400 sq mi) of the surface may be in permanent shadow.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-M03-119) The presence of usable quantities of water on the Moon is an important factor in rendering [lunar habitation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon "Colonization of the Moon") as a cost-effective plan; the alternative of transporting water from Earth would be prohibitively expensive.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-seedhouse2009-170)

In years since, signatures of water have been found to exist on the lunar surface.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-moonwater_18032010-171) In 1994, the [bistatic radar experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clementine_mission#Bistatic_Radar_Experiment "Clementine mission") located on the _[Clementine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clementine\_(spacecraft) "Clementine (spacecraft)")_ spacecraft, indicated the existence of small, frozen pockets of water close to the surface. However, later radar observations by [Arecibo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arecibo_Telescope "Arecibo Telescope"), suggest these findings may rather be rocks ejected from young impact craters.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-172) In 1998, the [neutron spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Prospector#Neutron_Spectrometer_(NS) "Lunar Prospector") on the _Lunar Prospector_ spacecraft showed that high concentrations of hydrogen are present in the first meter of depth in the regolith near the polar regions.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Feldman1998-173) Volcanic lava beads, brought back to Earth aboard Apollo 15, showed small amounts of water in their interior.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Saal2008-174)

The 2008 _[Chandrayaan-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandrayaan-1 "Chandrayaan-1")_ spacecraft has since confirmed the existence of surface water ice, using the on-board [Moon Mineralogy Mapper](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon_Mineralogy_Mapper "Moon Mineralogy Mapper"). The spectrometer observed absorption lines common to [hydroxyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydroxyl "Hydroxyl"), in reflected sunlight, providing evidence of large quantities of water ice, on the lunar surface. The spacecraft showed that concentrations may possibly be as high as 1,000[ppm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parts_per_million "Parts per million").[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Pieters2009-175) Using the mapper's reflectance spectra, indirect lighting of areas in shadow confirmed water ice within 20¬∞ latitude of both poles in 2018.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-176) In 2009, _[LCROSS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LCROSS "LCROSS")_ sent a 2,300 kg (5,100 lb) impactor into a [permanently shadowed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permanently_shadowed_crater "Permanently shadowed crater") polar crater, and detected at least 100 kg (220 lb) of water in a plume of ejected material.[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Planetary-177)[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Colaprete-178) Another examination of the LCROSS data showed the amount of detected water to be closer to 155¬±12 kg (342¬±26 lb).[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-Colaprete2010-179)

In May 2011, 615‚Äì1410 ppm water in [melt inclusions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melt_inclusions "Melt inclusions") in lunar sample 74220 was reported,[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon#cite_note-hauri-180) the famous high-titanium "orange glass soil" of volcanic origin collected during the [Apollo 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_17 "Apollo 17") mission in 1972. The inclusions were formed during explosive eruptions on the Moon approximately 3.7 billion years ago. This concentration is comparable with that of magma in Earth's [upper mantle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_mantle "Upper mantle").
